Excerpt of Problem

From: https://adventofcode.com/2024/day/11


--- Day 11: Plutonian Pebbles ---

The ancient civilization on Pluto was known for its ability to manipulate spacetime, and while The Historians explore their infinite corridors, you've noticed a strange set of physics-defying stones.

At first glance, they seem like normal stones: they're arranged in a perfectly straight line, and each stone has a number engraved on it.

The strange part is that every time you blink, the stones change.

Sometimes, the number engraved on a stone changes. Other times, a stone might split in two, causing all the other stones to shift over a bit to make room in their perfectly straight line.

As you observe them for a while, you find that the stones have a consistent behavior. Every time you blink, the stones each simultaneously change according to the first applicable rule in this list:

If the stone is engraved with the number 0, it is replaced by a stone engraved with the number 1.
If the stone is engraved with a number that has an even number of digits, it is replaced by two stones. The left half of the digits are engraved on the new left stone, and the right half of the digits are engraved on the new right stone. (The new numbers don't keep extra leading zeroes: 1000 would become stones 10 and 0.)
If none of the other rules apply, the stone is replaced by a new stone; the old stone's number multiplied by 2024 is engraved on the new stone.

No matter how the stones change, their order is preserved, and they stay on their perfectly straight line.

How will the stones evolve if you keep blinking at them? You take a note of the number engraved on each stone in the line (your puzzle input).

If you have an arrangement of five stones engraved with the numbers 0 1 10 99 999 and you blink once, the stones transform as follows:

The first stone, 0, becomes a stone marked 1.
The second stone, 1, is multiplied by 2024 to become 2024.
The third stone, 10, is split into a stone marked 1 followed by a stone marked 0.
The fourth stone, 99, is split into two stones marked 9.
The fifth stone, 999, is replaced by a stone marked 2021976.

So, after blinking once, your five stones would become an arrangement of seven stones engraved with the numbers 1 2024 1 0 9 9 2021976.

Here is a longer example:

Initial arrangement:
125 17

After 1 blink:
253000 1 7

After 2 blinks:
253 0 2024 14168

After 3 blinks:
512072 1 20 24 28676032

After 4 blinks:
512 72 2024 2 0 2 4 2867 6032

After 5 blinks:
1036288 7 2 20 24 4048 1 4048 8096 28 67 60 32

After 6 blinks:
2097446912 14168 4048 2 0 2 4 40 48 2024 40 48 80 96 2 8 6 7 6 0 3 2

In this example, after blinking six times, you would have 22 stones. After blinking 25 times, you would have 55312 stones!

Consider the arrangement of stones in front of you. How many stones will you have after blinking 25 times?
 // input_data is a string of groups of decimal digits separated by whitespace. const { input_data } = await import_local('./input-data.js'); if (typeof input_data !== 'string') { throw new Error('input_data is not a string'); } this.input_data = input_data.trim(); if (!this.input_data.match(/^[\d]+(?:[\s]+[\d]+)*$/)) { throw new Error(`input contains nondigit/non-whitespace character(s): ${this.input_data}`); }
// utility definitions this.stone_output_options = { style: { 'font-family': 'monospace', 'white-space': 'nowrap', 'white-space-collapse': 'preserve', // 'max-height': '20ch', 'max-width': 'fit-content', padding: '1em', overflow: 'auto', border: '1px solid lightgrey', }, }; const TOO_LARGE_INTEGER = 2**53; this.init_stones = () => { //============================================================================================== // TRANSITION RULES: // // * If the stone is engraved with the number 0, it is replaced by a stone engraved with the // number 1. // // * If the stone is engraved with a number that has an even number of digits, it is replaced // by two stones. The left half of the digits are engraved on the new left stone, and the // right half of the digits are engraved on the new right stone. (The new numbers don't keep // extra leading zeroes: 1000 would become stones 10 and 0.) // // * If none of the other rules apply, the stone is replaced by a new stone; the old stone's // number multiplied by 2024 is engraved on the new stone. // //============================================================================================== // Argument for why arbitrary-precision arithmetic is not necessary when using these rules // // (In the following, "number of digits" refers to the number of decimal digits.) // // * numbers with an even number of digits are decreased drastically by the rules. // * the concern would be that, for some positive integer n with an odd number of digits, // repeated multiplications would continue to give values with an odd number of digits. // * Given a positive integer n, floor(log10(n)) is odd iff n has an even number of // digits. // * log10(2024) is approximately 3.3062105081677613. Note that floor(log10(2024)) is odd. // * therefore, multiplying positive integer n with an odd number of digits (and therefore // floor(log10(n)) is even) by 2024 will: // (1) produce a result with an odd number of digits if frac(log10(n)) >= 1-frac(log10(2024) // (which is approximately 0.6937894918322387) because [even]+[odd]+1 = [even] // (2) produce a result with an even number of digits if frac(log10(n)) < 1-frac(log10(2024) // In case (2), we are out of trouble because the rules will reduce the number. However, in // case (1) frac(log10(result)) will be less than floor(log10(2024)) and therefore another // multiplication by 2024 will produce a number with an even number of digits and again // we are out of trouble. (reason: frac(log10(2024)) < 0.5) // // Therefore, it is only necessary to limit the input numbers to be less than // TOO_LARGE_INTEGER/2024/2024 to ensure that we will not overflow. //============================================================================================== // these values are fairly conservative... this.MAX_INPUT_NUMBER = Math.floor(TOO_LARGE_INTEGER/2024/2024); this.MAX_INPUT_DIGITS = Math.floor(Math.log10(this.MAX_INPUT_NUMBER)); this.stones = this.input_data.split(/[\s]+/) .map(number_string => { if (number_string.length > this.MAX_INPUT_DIGITS) { // this is to ensure we don't need arbitrary-preciscion arithmetic... throw new Error(`rejecting input number ${number_string} because it has more than ${this.MAX_INPUT_DIGITS} digits`); } return +number_string; }); this.stone_blink_transition = (stone) => { // returns an array of new stones that will replace the input stone if (!Number.isInteger(stone) || stone < 0) { throw new Error(`stone is not a positive integer: ${stone}`); } if (stone === 0) { return [ 1 ]; } else { const stone_digits = stone.toString(); if (stone_digits.length % 2 === 0) { const mid = stone_digits.length / 2; return [ stone_digits.slice(0, mid), stone_digits.slice(mid) ].map(digits => +digits); } else { return [ (stone * 2024) ]; } } }; this.blink_transition = () => { return this.stones = this.stones.flatMap(this.stone_blink_transition); }; this.output_stones = async (ocx) => { return ocx.render_text(this.stones.join(' '), this.stone_output_options); }; };
 // show initial input data and calculate results await ocx.markdown(` # The initial input data: ## ${this.input_data} `);

The initial input data:

2 77706 5847 9258441 0 741 883933 12

 // calculate and display the answer to Part One const blink_count = 25; this.init_stones(); await ocx.println(`The initial stones (${this.stones.length}):`); await this.output_stones(ocx); for (let i = 0; i < blink_count; i++) { this.blink_transition(); } await println(); await ocx.println(`The stones after ${blink_count} blink${blink_count === 1 ? '' : 's'}:`); await this.output_stones(ocx); const answer_element = await ocx.markdown(` # Answer to Part One The number of stones after ${blink_count} blink${blink_count === 1 ? '' : 's'} is **${this.stones.length}**. `); // scroll answer_element into view, but after a delay to let other rendering finish sleep(0.1).then(() => answer_element.scrollIntoView());

Answer to Part One

The number of stones after 25 blinks is 190865.

--- Part Two ---

The Historians sure are taking a long time. To be fair, the infinite corridors are very large.

How many stones would you have after blinking a total of 75 times?

// definitions for Part Two with memoization const perform_blink_on_stone = (on_stone) => { // returns array of stones if (on_stone === 0) { return [ 1 ]; } else { const on_stone_digits = on_stone.toString(); if (on_stone_digits.length % 2 === 0) { const mid = on_stone_digits.length / 2; return [ +on_stone_digits.slice(0, mid), +on_stone_digits.slice(mid), ]; } else { return [ (on_stone * 2024) ]; } } }; let answers = {}; // stone -> array (indexed by blinks) of { stone, blinks, stones } // stones is an object mapping stone -> count // note that if answers[stone][blinks] exists then all the answers[stone][0...blinks-1] will also exist const reset_answers = () => { answers = {}; } const get_largest_partial_answer = (for_stone, desired_blinks, max_blinks) => { // returns largest answer with blinks <= desired_blinks, blinks will be at least min(1, desired_blinks) const answers_for_stone = (answers[for_stone] ??= new Array(max_blinks).fill()); let largest_partial_answer = answers_for_stone.findLast(answer => answer && (answer.blinks <= desired_blinks)); if (!largest_partial_answer) { // fill in the trivial base case largest_partial_answer = answers_for_stone[0] = { stone: for_stone, blinks: 0, stones: { [for_stone]: 1 } }; // also fill in first iteration const stones = {}; for (const b_stone of perform_blink_on_stone(for_stone)) { stones[b_stone] ??= 0; stones[b_stone] += 1; } answers_for_stone[1] = { stone: for_stone, blinks: 1, stones }; if (desired_blinks >= 1) { largest_partial_answer = answers_for_stone[1]; } } return largest_partial_answer; }; const multiply_stone_counts_object = (factor, stone_counts_object) => { const result = {}; for (const stone_string in stone_counts_object) { result[stone_string] = factor * stone_counts_object[stone_string]; } return result; }; const combine_stone_counts_objects = (stone_counts_objects) => { const combined = {}; for (const stone_counts_object of stone_counts_objects) { for (const stone_string in stone_counts_object) { combined[stone_string] ??= 0; combined[stone_string] += stone_counts_object[stone_string]; } } return combined;
